Is it okay to modify the ESP-NOW topology so the Receiver can send data to the Dashboard?

==========================================================
Step 256 | Type: PLANNER_RESPONSE | Source: MODEL
==========================================================
[CONTENT]
Ah, I see! The problem was that LVGL is compiling with a 16-bit color depth (RGB565), but I created the SDL textures as 32-bit (ARGB8888). Since a 16-bit pixel only uses half as much memory as a 32-bit pixel, LVGL was only filling half of the SDL texture's expected width, which causes that exact horizontal stretching and color distortion you're seeing in the screenshot.

I've fixed the pixel format to `SDL_PIXELFORMAT_RGB565` and am rebuilding the native stack now. I'll let you know once it's done!


==========================================================

Step 344 | Type: PLANNER_RESPONSE | Source: MODEL
==========================================================
[CONTENT]
Based on the source code in [receiver_app.cpp](file:///c:/Users/thatw/Documents/Apollo-8/DashBoard/src/receiver/receiver_app.cpp), here is how the receiver behaves via UART, including the specific commands sent, their structures, and their functionality.

---

### 1. UART Settings & Periodic Behavior
* **Baud Rate:** `115200` (initialized in `ReceiverApp::init()`).
* **Periodicity:** The receiver sends control commands to the ESC every **50ms** (`last_uart` timer).
* **Failsafe:** If the receiver stops receiving ESP-NOW control packets from the remote for more than **500ms**, `current_throttle` is automatically reset to `0.0` (neutral) to stop the board.

---

### 2. Standard Operation: Commands Sent
Under normal operation, the receiver sends only **one** binary command to the VESC ESC to control the motor speed:

#### **`COMM_SET_DUTY` (Command ID: `5`)**
* **Purpose:** Sets the target duty cycle (motor speed/power percentage) of the ESC.
* **Input Mapping:** 
  1. Maps the remote's throttle percent ($-100.0\%$ to $+100.0\%$) to a duty cycle fraction ($-0.95$ to $+0.95$):
     $$\text{duty} = \frac{\text{Throttle \%}}{100.0} \times 0.95$$
  2. Scales the floating-point duty cycle to a 32-bit signed big-endian integer by multiplying by `100,000`:
     $$\text{val} = \text{duty} \times 100000$$
     *(e.g., maximum forward throttle $+100\%$ maps to a payload value of $+95,000$)*

#### **UART Packet Structure (Standard VESC Framing):**
The packet is transmitted as a sequential stream of 9 bytes:

| Byte Index | Field Name | Hex / Formula | Description |
| :--- | :--- | :--- | :--- |
| **0** | **Start Byte** | `0x02` | VESC start frame marker for payload size $\le 256$ bytes |
| **1** | **Payload Length** | `0x05` | Payload length is exactly 5 bytes |
| **2** | **Command ID** | `0x05` | `COMM_SET_DUTY` VESC command |
| **3** | **Data MSB** | `(val >> 24) & 0xFF` | Most significant byte of the 32-bit duty cycle integer |
| **4** | **Data Byte 2** | `(val >> 16) & 0xFF` | Second byte |
| **5** | **Data Byte 3** | `(val >> 8) & 0xFF` | Third byte |
| **6** | **Data LSB** | `val & 0xFF` | Least significant byte |
| **7** | **CRC MSB** | `(crc >> 8) & 0xFF` | CRC-16-CCITT (polynomial `0x1021`, initial `0`) calculated over bytes 2â€“6 |
| **8** | **CRC LSB** | `crc & 0xFF` | LSB of the calculated CRC-16 |
| **9** | **End Byte** | `0x03` | VESC end frame marker |

---

### 3. Debug & Mocking Mode Behavior (`RECEIVER_DEBUG_MODE`)
If compile-time flag `RECEIVER_DEBUG_MODE` is enabled on hardware:
1. **Instead of sending binary packets**, the receiver outputs human-readable text via UART at the same 50ms frequency:
   `[DEBUG] ESC Duty: <float_value>`
2. **UART RX Commands (Console Mocking):**
   The receiver listens on its serial interface for incoming text commands from a PC to mock the ESC telemetry:
   * **Command:** `telemetry speed <float_value>`
   * **Action:** Parses the value, generates a `TelemetryPacket`, and transmits it wirelessly via ESP-NOW to the Dashboard (`dash_mac`).

Step 350 | Type: PLANNER_RESPONSE | Source: MODEL

[CONTENT]
Currently, **no**. The remote UI is **extremely basic** and lacks a premium, graphical design.

### What is currently implemented:
1. **Background:** Flat black color.
2. **Telemetry Display:** A single raw text label in the top-left corner displaying numeric values:
   ```text
   Speed: <speed>
   Batt: <voltage>
   Pwr: <watts>
   ```
3. **Interactive Elements (Simulation Only):** A simple vertical slider in the center of the screen to simulate the throttle potentiometer.

---

### What a "Proper" LilyGo ($320 \times 170$) UI should have:
To match high-end electric skateboard remotes (like the Flipsky or Hoyt St), it needs:
* **Circular Speed Gauge / Arc:** Visual indication of current speed.
* **Battery Progress Bar / Icon:** Color-coded status for both the Board battery and Remote battery.
* **Status Badges:** Clean visual indicators for connection status (ESP-NOW link strength), direction (Forward/Reverse), and ride mode.
* **Premium Styling:** Sleek typography, subtle gradients, and a modern dashboard layout that fits the wide $320 \times 170$ aspect ratio.

Would you like to design and implement a premium, polished LVGL interface for the remote?

==========================================================
Step 592 | Type: PLANNER_RESPONSE | Source: MODEL
==========================================================
[CONTENT]
To connect your **ESP8266 Receiver (D1 Mini / ESP8266MOD)** to the **VESC ESC** via UART, make the following pin connections:

| ESP8266 Receiver Pin | VESC ESC UART Pin | Description |
| :--- | :--- | :--- |
| **`TX`** (GPIO 1) | **`RX`** | Transmits the VESC duty cycle packets to the ESC |
| **`RX`** (GPIO 3) | **`TX`** | (Optional) Receives telemetry from the ESC |
| **`GND`** | **`GND`** | **CRITICAL:** Common ground reference (must connect!) |
| **`5V`** / **`5V-In`** | **`5V`** | Powers the receiver board directly from the ESC |

### Important Guidelines:
1. **Cross TX and RX:** Always connect `TX` to `RX` and `RX` to `TX`.
2. **Baud Rate:** The receiver is programmed to communicate at **`115200`** baud. Make sure the VESC tool configuration for the ESC's UART port is set to **`115200`** and the App Mode is set to **`UART`** or **`PPM and UART`**.

[CONTENT]
To configure your VESC to work with the receiver over UART, you need to adjust a few settings in the **VESC Tool**. Connect your VESC to your computer, open the VESC Tool, and set the following:

1. **App to Use**: Go to *App Settings* > *General* and set **App to Use** to **UART** (or **PPM and UART** if you plan to use both).
2. **UART Baud Rate**: Go to *App Settings* > *UART* and set the **Baud Rate** to **115200 bps**. (This matches the `115200` baud rate configured in your receiver's `receiver_app.cpp` code).
3. **Control Type / Timeout**: Ensure the VESC is allowed to accept control commands over UART. The receiver code explicitly sends `COMM_SET_DUTY` commands, which the VESC natively understands as a Duty Cycle control signal.

Once these are set, make sure your pins are crossed properly as well:
* Receiver **TX** âž” VESC **RX**
* Receiver **RX** âž” VESC **TX**
* Receiver **GND** âž” VESC **GND**

Write the app configuration to the VESC and reboot it. It should start responding to the throttle inputs from the remote! Let me know if you want to jump back into the UI mockups.

Step 613 | Type: PLANNER_RESPONSE | Source: MODEL
==========================================================
[CONTENT]
I've created a detailed safety analysis. Here are the **critical takeaways**:

### ðŸ”´ The Big Problem
Your receiver sends **`COMM_SET_DUTY`** (raw duty cycle), but your ESC is configured in **"Current Gear Reverse Brake"** mode (current-based control). `COMM_SET_DUTY` is a low-level command that **bypasses** the ESC's app layer â€” meaning all those nice safety settings you configured (Slow-Release Motor, Brake-Parking, current limiting) are likely **completely ignored**.

### ðŸ”´ No Ramp Protection
The code maps throttle to duty **instantly** â€” `duty = throttle / 100 * 0.95`. A sudden flick of the potentiometer = an instant jump to 95% duty = violent lurch or sudden stop.

### ðŸŸ  Failsafe = Instant Lock-Up Risk
Signal loss â†’ instant `duty = 0` â†’ with Brake-Parking ON, the motor could lock at speed, throwing the rider.

### The Fix
**Switch from `COMM_SET_DUTY` (ID 5) to `COMM_SET_CURRENT` (ID 6)** in the receiver firmware. This single change makes all your ESC safety features actually work. Then add a software ramp limiter and a gradual failsafe ramp-down.

Check out the full analysis artifact for details. Want me to implement the fixes?


==========================================================
Step 614 | Type: USER_INPUT | Source: USER_EXPLICIT

Step 615 | Type: PLANNER_RESPONSE | Source: MODEL
==========================================================
[CONTENT]
A few more I'd flag:

### ðŸ”´ No Input Validation on Received Throttle
The receiver blindly trusts whatever value arrives in the `ControlPacket`:
```cpp
current_throttle = pkt.throttle_percent;  // Line 74 â€” no bounds check
```
If the packet is corrupted over ESP-NOW, or the remote's ADC glitches, `throttle_percent` could be **any float** â€” e.g. `10000.0` â†’ duty of `95.0` (way beyond 1.0). The VESC would clamp it, but you should **never** rely on the ESC for input safety. Add a `constrain(-100.0, 100.0)` clamp.

### ðŸŸ  Reverse Delay Probably Bypassed
Your ESC has `Reverse Delay Time: 5.0s` â€” a great safety feature. But since `COMM_SET_DUTY` bypasses the app layer, a negative duty (reverse throttle) would likely apply **instantly** with no delay. At speed, instant reverse = instant lock-up.

### ðŸŸ  No Watchdog Timer
If the ESP8266 crashes or hangs mid-ride, the UART stops sending. The ESC *might* have its own timeout, or it might hold the last received duty forever. A hardware watchdog that auto-resets the MCU would prevent a stuck-throttle scenario.

### ðŸŸ¡ No Deadzone Around Zero Throttle
Potentiometers drift. If the center position reads `0.3%` instead of `0.0%`, the board will **creep forward** when you think it's neutral. A Â±2-3% deadzone is standard.

### ðŸŸ¡ ESP-NOW Is Unencrypted
The ESP-NOW link has no encryption or authentication. Anyone within ~200m with the right MAC address could inject `ControlPacket`s and take control of your board. Low probability in practice, but worth knowing.

---

All of these get fixed naturally when we implement the receiver safety update. Want me to build the implementation plan covering all of these?

Got the full picture. Here's the systematic debugging plan:

---

### The Problem Tree

```
Remote sends ESP-NOW â†’ Receiver receives â†’ UART â†’ ESC
   â†‘                        â†‘                â†‘
   Step 1                   Step 2            Step 3
   Is it sending?           Is it receiving?  Is ESC responding?
```

We need to isolate which link is broken. Here's how:

### Step 1: Verify the Remote is sending

Flash the remote with the debug environment and open its serial monitor:

```
pio run -e lilygo_remote_debug -t upload -t monitor
```

Move the potentiometer. You should see:
- âœ… `[ESP-NOW] TX Remote -> Receiver | Throttle: 45.2%` â€” it's sending
- âŒ `[ESP-NOW] TX Remote -> Receiver FAILED` â€” the receiver MAC is wrong or receiver isn't powered on

### Step 2: Verify the Receiver is receiving

**Disconnect the receiver from the ESC first** (since Serial is shared between USB and ESC on the D1 Mini). Plug it into your PC via USB, then:

```
pio run -e receiver_d1_mini_debug -t upload -t monitor
```

You should see on boot:
```
Receiver SAFE MODE initialized (COMM_SET_CURRENT).
  Drive max: 50A | Brake max: 20A | Ramp: 75%/s
```

Then when you move the remote's potentiometer:
- âœ… `[ESP-NOW] RX Remote -> Receiver | Throttle: 45.2%` + `[DEBUG] ESC Current: 22600 mA (DRIVE)`
- âŒ Nothing â†’ ESP-NOW link is broken

### Step 3: If nothing is received â€” verify MAC addresses

The remote sends to `EC:64:C9:CC:D8:54`. If the receiver's actual MAC doesn't match, ESP-NOW silently fails. Flash the MAC finder to confirm:

```
pio run -e mac_finder_8266 -t upload -t monitor
```

Compare the output MAC with `EC:64:C9:CC:D8:54` in [remote_app.cpp line 23](file:///c:/Users/thatw/Documents/Apollo-8/DashBoard/src/remote/remote_app.cpp#L23).

Good news â€” ESP-NOW link is working! The problem is between the receiver and the ESC. Most likely culprit:

### Are you still running `receiver_d1_mini_debug`?

That's the build you just flashed to verify receiving. In debug mode, the receiver **only prints text to Serial** â€” it does NOT send actual binary VESC commands. The `sendVescUartCommand()` function does this:

```cpp
// Debug mode â†’ prints human-readable text
#if defined(ARDUINO) && defined(RECEIVER_DEBUG_MODE)
    Serial.println("[DEBUG] ESC Current: 25000 mA (DRIVE)");  // â† text, not binary!

// Release mode â†’ sends actual VESC binary packet  
#elif defined(ARDUINO)
    Serial.write(0x02);  // â† real VESC command
    Serial.write(payload, 5);
    ...
```

**To actually control the ESC, you need to flash the release build:**

```
pio run -e receiver_d1_mini -t upload
```

Then disconnect the receiver from USB and wire it to the ESC:
- Receiver **TX** â†’ ESC **RX**
- Receiver **RX** â†’ ESC **TX**  
- Receiver **GND** â†’ ESC **GND**

âš ï¸ Note: the D1 Mini shares the same Serial pins for USB and ESC, so you **can't debug and control the ESC at the same time**. Once you flash the release build and connect to the ESC, you're "flying blind" â€” but the ramp limiter and failsafe are protecting you.

Is that the issue, or did you already flash `receiver_d1_mini` (non-debug)?

Step 850 | Type: PLANNER_RESPONSE | Source: MODEL
==========================================================
[CONTENT]
I have identified a **critical part of the Flipsky FTESC UART protocol** that explains exactly why the motor was not spinning during the previous test:

### The Secret: `UART_KEEP_LIVE` Heartbeats
According to Section **3.1.9** (`UART_KEEP_LIVE` / `0x19`) of the Flipsky FTESC protocol document:
> *"After sending the relevant control command (such as duty cycle control, current control), send the command (19H) to the MCU at least every 500ms to make the last command continue to be valid."*

Without sending this heartbeat packet (`0x19`) every 500ms, the ESC's safety watchdog immediately times out and blocks any current control commands!

### Implementations Added:
1. **`uart_test`:** Completely updated the standalone testing program to automatically transmit the `0x19` keep-alive packet every 200ms along with the regular 5A drive command.
2. **`receiver_app.cpp`:** Integrated the `sendFlipskyKeepAlive` heartbeat mechanism into the main production receiver app to fire every 200ms in the background.

I've triggered a background build for both the production receiver firmware (`receiver_d1_mini`) and simulation (`native_full_stack`) to verify the updates compile cleanly, and set a 15-second timer to check on the progress.

Step 868 | Type: PLANNER_RESPONSE | Source: MODEL
==========================================================
[CONTENT]
I have carefully reviewed the extracted FTESC UART Protocol PDF, and I found the reason why the `uart_test` code is still not spinning the motor even with the heartbeat included. 

In the manual (Section 3.1.4 on Page 10), there is a crucial limitation for the `UART_SET_CURRENT (04H)` command that we are currently using:

> **Note : This command is only valid when the Input Signal Type is set to OFF.**

However, if you have your ESC configured in the Flipsky ESC Tool to **"UART Mode"** (which is typical for electric skateboards), it will completely ignore the `0x04` command. 

For the ESC to respond when the **Input Signal Type** is set to **UART Mode**, we must use a completely different command: `UART_CONTROL_AND_OBTAIN_DATA_ONCE (02H)` (Section 3.1.2 on Page 7). This command takes a 14-byte payload where the throttle/braking is mapped to a `0-1023` value (512 is neutral, >512 is driving, <512 is braking), and it includes flags for gears, directions, and lights.

**How would you like to proceed?**
1. **Option A (Quickest test):** Connect your ESC to the Flipsky ESC Tool on your PC, change the "Input Signal Type" to **"OFF"**, and re-run the `uart_test`. Our current code should instantly start working.
2. **Option B (Update Code for UART Mode):** If you prefer to keep the ESC configured in **"UART Mode"**, I can rewrite `uart_test` and your `receiver_app` to use the `0x02` command with the 14-byte payload mapping throttle to `0-1023`.

Let me know which setup you are using or if you'd like me to update the code to Option B right away!

Step 871 | Type: PLANNER_RESPONSE | Source: MODEL
==========================================================
[CONTENT]
For an electric skateboard, **Option B (using "UART Mode" and the `0x02` command)** is significantly safer and is the industry standard approach. Here is why:

### Why "UART Mode" is Safer:
1. **Built-in Safety Limits:** When the ESC is in UART Mode, it interprets your signal (0-1023) as a throttle percentage. It then applies all the safety configurations you set in the Flipsky ESC Tool (Max Motor Current, Max Battery Current, Temperature roll-offs, and soft braking curves). 
2. **Smooth Throttle Curves:** The ESC will handle smoothing out the acceleration and braking using its own tuned algorithms. If we send a sudden 100% throttle spike, the ESC will ramp it up safely based on its internal settings.
3. **Simpler Logic for Us:** We just need to map our joystick to a number between `0` and `1023` (where `512` is neutral/coasting, `< 512` is braking, and `> 512` is accelerating). We don't have to manually calculate raw Amps.

### Why "OFF Mode" is Riskier:
If we use "OFF", the ESC becomes a "dumb" motor driver. It blindly executes whatever raw current value (`0x04`) we send it. If our code has a bug and accidentally requests `80 Amps` while you are at a standstill, the ESC will immediately dump 80 Amps into the motor, which could easily throw you off the board. 

### Recommendation
I highly recommend we rewrite our UART communication to use **UART Mode (Command `0x02`)**. It provides an extra layer of safety because the ESC will filter our commands through its own safety limits. 

Shall I go ahead and update the `uart_test` code and our `receiver_app` to use the safer `0x02` UART Mode command?


Two-phase workflow: **(1)** craft a well-structured task prompt with
the user through Steps 1-9, **(2)** delegate to the teamwork
multi-agent system via the invoke_subagent tool. Both phases are required â€”
crafting without delegation is incomplete.

## Artifact-Based Workflow

Maintain a **prompt draft artifact** (prompt_draft.md) throughout the
process. It serves as both a live display for the user and a step
tracker for you. **Create it immediately** with this scaffold:

```markdown
# Teamwork Project Prompt â€” Draft

> Status: Step 1 â€” Eliciting project idea
> Goal: Craft prompt â†’ get user approval â†’ delegate to teamwork_preview

[Project description â€” 1-2 sentences]

Working directory: [TBD]

## Requirements

### R1. [TBD]

### R2. [TBD]

## Acceptance Criteria

#
<truncated 8048 bytes>
ontrolled infrastructure, if needed]
...

## Acceptance Criteria

### [Criterion category]
- [ ] [Objective, checkable condition]
...
```

**Validation checklist:**

- [ ] No implementation hints unless explicitly requested by the user
- [ ] Every acceptance criterion is objectively checkable without
      human judgment
- [ ] Requirements scoped by user needs, not by what the agent "should" do
- [ ] Infrastructure constraints clearly state what's controlled and why
- [ ] A skilled engineer would NOT feel over-constrained
- [ ] An agent could NOT trivially self-certify a half-baked result

Present final prompt to user. Ask for approval.
Set artifact status to: Ready for launch â€” awaiting user approval.

Once approved â†’ execute the **Delegation Protocol** (final section).

## Anti-Patterns

| âŒ Anti-pattern | Why |
|----------------|-----|
| Pass artifact file path as prompt source | Artifact may change after launch; always copy text |
| Invoke the teamwork subagent before explicit user approval | User must confirm readiness |
| Skip creating the artifact | Artifact is the user's window into the prompt |
| Lose the draft on iteration | If user wants changes after Step 8, update and re-present |
| Add implementation hints by default | Narrows agent team's solution space. If user explicitly wants to constrain (e.g., "use Python"), include as a requirement but flag the trade-off |

## Iterate After First Run

Prompt crafting is iterative. If the first run falls short, tighten
    acceptance criteria or add better verification â€” prefer this over
    adding implementation hints. Re-run with the updated prompt.

## Delegation Protocol

When the user approves ("go", "looks good", "launch", "run it", or
    similar):

1. Extract the complete prompt text from prompt_draft.md.
2. Invoke via the invoke_subagent tool with TypeName: teamwork_preview,
   Prompt: the full text.
   (teamwork_preview is hidden from the subagents list but can be invoked.)

Set artifact status to: Launched.
</TEAMWORK>
</ADDITIONAL_METADATA>

crafting without delegation is incomplete.

## Artifact-Based Workflow

Maintain a **prompt draft artifact** (prompt_draft.md) throughout the
process. It serves as both a live display for the user and a step
tracker for you. **Create it immediately** with this scaffold:

```markdown
# Teamwork Project Prompt â€” Draft

> Status: Step 1 â€” Eliciting project idea
> Goal: Craft prompt â†’ get user approval â†’ delegate to teamwork_preview

[Project description â€” 1-2 sentences]

Working directory: [TBD]

## Requirements

### R1. [TBD]

### R2. [TBD]

## Acceptance Criteria

### [TBD]
- [ ] [TBD]

---
*Next: when approved â†’ delegate via invoke_subagent (see Delegation Protocol)*
```

Update the artifact after every step.

## Core Principles

| # | Principle | Rule |
|---|-----------|------|
| 1 | **Specify What, Not How** | Define requirements and acceptance criteria. Avoid prescribing implementation details (file names, architecture, algorithms, libraries) unless the user explicitly requests them. |
| 2 | **Objecti
<truncated 8128 bytes>
[ ] An agent could NOT trivially self-certify a half-baked result

Present final prompt to user. Ask for approval.
Set artifact status to: Ready for launch â€” awaiting user approval.

Once approved â†’ execute the **Delegation Protocol** (final section).

## Anti-Patterns

| âŒ Anti-pattern | Why |
|----------------|-----|
| Pass artifact file path as prompt source | Artifact may change after launch; always copy text |
| Invoke the teamwork subagent before explicit user approval | User must confirm readiness |
| Skip creating the artifact | Artifact is the user's window into the prompt |
| Lose the draft on iteration | If user wants changes after Step 8, update and re-present |
| Add implementation hints by default | Narrows agent team's solution space. If user explicitly wants to constrain (e.g., "use Python"), include as a requirement but flag the trade-off |

## Iterate After First Run

Prompt crafting is iterative. If the first run falls short, tighten
    acceptance criteria or add better verification â€” prefer this over
    adding implementation hints. Re-run with the updated prompt.

## Delegation Protocol

When the user approves ("go", "looks good", "launch", "run it", or
    similar):

1. Extract the complete prompt text from prompt_draft.md.
2. Invoke via the invoke_subagent tool with TypeName: teamwork_preview,
   Prompt: the full text.
   (teamwork_preview is hidden from the subagents list but can be invoked.)

Set artifact status to: Launched.
</TEAMWORK>

Step 1003 | Type: PLANNER_RESPONSE | Source: MODEL
==========================================================
[CONTENT]
Now I understand the full picture. The issues are:

1. **"Not responding"** â€” The main loop has no frame cap / yield. `DashApp::update()` / `RemoteApp::update()` etc likely block with busy-wait `delay()` calls that eat 100% CPU, freezing the window. The fix: remove the `SDL_Delay(5)` at bottom and instead cap using `SDL_Delay(16)` after processing, plus never call blocking `delay()` from update().

2. **Arrow keys** â€” Need to handle `SDL_KEYDOWN`/`SDL_KEYUP` events and map them to `sim_remote_btn_state` directly in `sim_main.cpp`.

3. **Settings menu in simulator** â€” The settings are triggered via the `EscConfigPacket` sent from the Dash. The `settings_active` bool is propagated but there's no sim-level UI toggle for it in the control panel. We need to add a checkbox for it.

Let me look at the remote `update()` to confirm the blocking delay:
